8 min
Sale of Mortgaged Property Without Permission: Suspended Validity
Ruling on sale of pledged property without mortgagee's consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of mortgaged property without the mortgagee's permission and apply the principle of bay' mawquf to determine legal outcomes.

Core Idea
The sale of mortgaged property without consent is suspended, not void, and becomes effective upon consent or debt repayment.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: what happens when a mortgagor sells the pledged property without the mortgagee's permission? The Hanafi school classifies this sale as suspended (mawquf), not void, due to the mortgagee's attached right. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly resolving disputes in transactions involving pledges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
From al-Quduri's matn: the sale of the pledge without the mortgagee's permission is suspended (mawquf). It is not immediately void or valid.
Bay' mawquf is a unique Hanafi category.
2
Understand the reason for suspension
The suspension is due to the attachment of the mortgagee's right (haqq al-murtahin) to the pledged property. Until that right is resolved, the sale cannot be effective.
Compare to the bequest of all property, which requires heirs' permission beyond one-third.
3
Analyze the conditions for effectiveness
The sale becomes effective (nafidh) in two ways: (1) the mortgagee grants permission, or (2) the mortgagor repays the debt, removing the impediment.
When permission is given, the mortgagee's right transfers to the price (thaman), not extinguished.
4
Examine the consequences if permission is denied
If the mortgagee does not permit and cancels the sale, it is void in one narration. The buyer has no claim even if the mortgagor later redeems the pledge.
The mortgagee's right is akin to ownership, giving him the power to permit or cancel.
5
Apply the qiyas (analogy) used
The ruling is analogized to a person bequeathing all his property: the heirs' right attaches, making the bequest suspended beyond one-third. Similarly, the mortgagee's right attaches to the pledge.
The illa (effective cause) is the attachment of another's right.

Ashraf al-Hidaya, Urdu commentary on Hidaya, Volume 14, page 277. Book of Pledge. Chapter on Disposition in the Pledge, Offenses against It, and Its Offense against Others. Commentary: In this chapter, the rulings on disposition in the pledge, offenses against the pledge, and if the pledged property commits an offense against someone will be explained. Since rulings on disposition and offense can only occur after the establishment of the thing, first the establishment of the pledge was discussed, and then after that, rulings on disposition and offense etc. were explained so that the arranged order matches the natural order. Ruling on the mortgagor selling the pledged property without the mortgagee's permission. He (al-Quduri) said: And if the mortgagor sells the pledge without the permission of the mortgagee, the sale is suspended because the right of another is attached to it, and that is the mortgagee, so it depends on his permission, even though the mortgagor is disposing of his own property, like one who bequeaths all his property; it depends on the permission of the heirs regarding what exceeds one-third because their right is attached to it. So if the mortgagee permits, it is valid because the suspension was for his right, and he has agreed to its removal. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And when the mortgagor sells the pledged property without the mortgagee's permission, the sale is suspended due to the attachment of another's right to the pledged object, and that is the mortgagee; thus the sale is suspended pending his permission, even though the mortgagor is disposing of his own property — like a person who bequeaths all his property; it is suspended on the permission of the heirs regarding what exceeds one-third due to the attachment of their right to the property. So if the mortgagee gives permission, the sale is valid because the suspension was due to his right, and he has agreed to its relinquishment. Commentary: The principle is that if someone's right becomes attached to a thing, then until that person to whose right the thing is attached gives permission, the owner's disposition in it will not be effective, just as when a person bequeaths all his property, since the heirs' right is attached to the property, for execution of the bequest beyond one-third, the permission of the heirs is required. Similarly, the mortgagee's right is attached to the pledged property, so until the mortgagee gives permission, the mortgagor's sale will not be effective but will remain suspended, even though the mortgagor is disposing of his own property. And when the mortgagee gives permission, the sale becomes valid because the suspension was due to the mortgagee's right, and the mortgagee has removed his right, so the sale becomes permissible. If the mortgagor repays the mortgagee's debt, the sale is also valid. And if the mortgagor repays his debt, it is also valid because the impediment to effectiveness has been removed, and the effective cause (muqtada) exists, which is the disposition proceeding from a qualified person in a proper place. Translation: And if the mortgagor repays the mortgagee's debt, the sale is also valid because the thing preventing the effectiveness of the sale has been removed, and the effective cause exists, which is the disposition from a qualified person in the proper place. Commentary: The mortgagee's right is attached to the pledged property due to the debt. If the mortgagor repays the mortgagee's debt, the impediment is removed, so the sale becomes valid. Question: With only the removal of the impediment, the issue is not resolved unless the effective cause exists? Answer: The effective cause already exists because the pledged property is a valid place for sale, as it is owned by the mortgagor, and the mortgagor, being sane and adult, is qualified for sale. So when the impediment is removed, the effective cause will operate. If the mortgagee gives permission for the sale, the mortgagee's right transfers to its substitute (badal). And when the sale is executed by the mortgagee's permission, his right transfers to its substitute — this is correct because his right is attached to the value (maliyyah), and the substitute has the same ruling as the substituted. So it becomes like when a slave in debt is sold with the consent of the creditors; their right transfers to the substitute because they agree to the transfer, not outright extinction. So too here. Translation: And when the sale becomes effective with the mortgagee's permission, the mortgagee's right transfers to its substitute — this is the correct view because his right is attached to the monetary value, and the substitute has the ruling of the substituted. So it becomes like when a slave in debt is sold with the consent of the creditors; their right transfers to the substitute because they agree to the transfer, not outright extinguishment. So too here. Commentary: When the sale becomes effective with the mortgagee's permission, the price (thaman) will take the place of the pledged property as a pledge because the mortgagee's right was attached to the actual pledged property in terms of its value, and just as the substituted (the pledge) was property, so too the substitute (the price) is property. Summary: The mortgagee's right does not become completely extinguished but rather transfers. Its example is exactly like when a slave in debt is sold by the owner with the consent of the creditors; the sale is valid, but the creditors' right is not extinguished but transfers — meaning previously their right was attached to the slave, now it becomes attached to his price. So too here. If the mortgagor does not repay the debt and the mortgagee does not permit, the sale is not executed. And if the mortgagee does not permit the sale and cancels it, it is cancelled in one narration, such that even if the mortgagor redeems the pledge, the buyer has no recourse because the right established for the mortgagee is in the position of ownership, so the mortgagee is like an owner — he has the right to permit and the right to cancel. Translation: And if the mortgagee does not permit the sale and cancels it, it is cancelled in one narration, such that even if the mortgagor redeems the pledge, the buyer has no recourse because the right established for the mortgagee is in the position of ownership, so the mortgagee is like an owner — he may permit or cancel.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the ruling when a mortgagor (raahin) sells the pledged property (marhun) without the mortgagee's (murtahin) permission. The text begins by stating the ruling from al-Quduri's matn: the sale is suspended (mawquf) due to the attachment of the mortgagee's right. The author then provides an analogy: just as a bequest of all property beyond one-third requires the heirs' permission because their right is attached, so too the sale of mortgaged property requires the mortgagee's permission. If the mortgagee permits, the sale becomes valid. Next, the text addresses another scenario: if the mortgagor repays the debt, the impediment is removed, and the sale becomes valid automatically because the effective cause (the disposition by a qualified person) already exists. The commentary answers a potential objection: the effective cause was always present; only the impediment was removed. The third point: if the mortgagee permits, his right does not become void but transfers to the price (thaman). The author compares this to a slave in debt being sold with the creditors' consent; their right transfers to the substitute. Finally, if the mortgagee does not permit and cancels the sale, it becomes void (in one narration). The mortgagee has the authority to permit or cancel because his right is akin to ownership. Overall, the passage clarifies that the mortgagee's right is a legally recognized encumbrance that limits the mortgagor's ownership, and any disposition by the mortgagor is conditional upon either the mortgagee's consent or the extinguishment of the debt.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): The contract of pledge or the property given as security for a debt.
راهن (raahin): The person who gives the pledge; the mortgagor.
مرتهن (murtahin): The person who receives the pledge; the mortgagee (creditor).
بیع موقوف (bay' mawquf): A sale that is not immediately effective but is suspended pending the fulfillment of a condition or consent of a third party whose right is attached.
بدل (badal): The substitute; here, the price (thaman) received from the sale of the pledged property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mortgagor sells the pledged property without the mortgagee's permission, what is the legal status of the sale according to the Hanafi school?
A) The sale is void (batil) immediately.
B) The sale is suspended (mawquf) pending the mortgagee's consent.
C) The sale is valid and the mortgagee's right is extinguished.
D) The sale is valid only if the debt is less than the value of the property.

Key Takeaway
The sale of mortgaged property without the mortgagee's permission is suspended (mawquf), not void, and becomes effective upon consent or debt repayment, with the mortgagee's right transferring to the price.

Sale of Mortgaged Property After Debt Repayment
Commentary on a ruling with aqli proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the sale of mortgaged property is valid after debt repayment, applying the concepts of manih and muqtada.

Core Idea
Removal of impediment activates the existing effective cause — the sale becomes valid.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah clarifies a key principle in the law of mortgage (rahn): once the mortgagor repays the debt, the mortgagee's lien dissolves, and the sale of the property becomes valid. The reasoning hinges on two concepts: the impediment (manih) that blocked the sale and the effective cause (muqtada) that enables it. Understanding this interplay is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rules on property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
Read the passage to determine the exact ruling: that the sale of mortgaged property is valid after the mortgagor repays the debt. Note that this is a specific case within the broader chapter of rahn.
The ruling is a clarification of a general principle: impediments block contracts, but when removed, the contract becomes effective if the cause exists.
2
Distinguish the impediment (manih) from the effective cause (muqtada)
Locate in the passage the terms 'manih' (impediment) and 'muqtada' (effective cause). Understand that the mortgagee's right is the impediment; the mortgagor's ownership and legal capacity form the effective cause.
The muqtada is always present — the impediment is the only obstacle.
3
Trace the removal of the impediment
Follow the logic: when the debt is paid, the mortgagee's lien dissolves. This removal is sufficient to let the existing effective cause operate. Confirm that the passage explicitly states: 'the thing that was an impediment has been removed.'
No additional action (like mortgagee's consent) is needed.
4
Apply the reasoning to a parallel scenario
Consider a parallel case: if a person pledges property for a debt and later sells it without repaying, the sale is suspended (mawquf) until the debtor repays or the mortgagee consents. After repayment, it becomes effective immediately. This shows the same principle at work.
The muqtada (ownership+capacity) is the same; only the manih changes.
5
Evaluate the difference with other schools
Note that some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require the mortgagee's consent even after repayment if the mortgagee still holds possession. The Hanafi position, as stated, does not require consent because the lien is a right that is satisfied by repayment.
This is a point of ikhtilaf; the thamarah al-khilaf is the need for formal consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴
ترجمہ اور اگر راہن نے مرتهن کا قرض ادا کر دیا تو بھی بیع جائز ہے اس لئے کہ جو چیز نفوذ بیع سے مانع تھی وہ زائل ہوگئی اور مقتضی موجود ہے اور وہ محل میں اہل کی جانب سے تصرف ہے۔
تشریح مرتہن کا حق مرہون کے ساتھ قرض کی وجہ سے متعلق ہے اگر راہن نے مرتهن کا قرض ادا کیا تو مانع ختم ہو گیا اس لئے بیع جائز ہوگی۔
سوال تنہا مانع کے زوال سے تو مسئلہ حل نہیں ہو گا جب تک کہ مقتضی موجود نہ ہو؟ جواب مقتضی پہلے سے موجود ہے کیونکہ مرہون محل بیع ہے اس لئے کہ وہ راہن کا مملوک ہے اور راہن عاقل بالغ ہونے کی وجہ بیع کا اہل ہے تو جب مانع ختم ہو گیا تو مقتضی اپنا کام کرے گا۔
Translation: And if the mortgagor pays off the debt of the mortgagee, the sale is valid, because the thing that was an impediment to the effectiveness of the sale has been removed, and the effective cause exists, and it is a disposal by the owner in his property. Clarification: The right of the mortgagee is attached to the mortgaged property due to the debt. If the mortgagor pays the mortgagee's debt, the impediment is removed, so the sale will be valid. Question: With merely the removal of the impediment, the issue is not resolved unless the effective cause is present? Answer: The effective cause is already present because the mortgaged property is the subject of sale, as it is owned by the mortgagor, and the mortgagor, being sane and adult, is qualified for sale. So when the impediment is removed, the effective cause will operate.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the sale of mortgaged property after the mortgagor repays the debt. The ruling states that such a sale is valid. The reasoning is based on the removal of the impediment (manih) — which is the mortgagee's right attached to the property — and the persistence of the effective cause (muqtada) for the sale, namely the ownership and legal capacity of the mortgagor. The commentary clarifies that the mortgagee's right is a lien on the property for the debt, and once the debt is paid, that lien dissolves. The seller (mortgagor) already has the authority to sell as owner, so the sale becomes effective. This ruling is consistent with Hanafi principles: the existence of a valid cause (sabab) and removal of impediment make the contract operative.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): The contract of pawning; giving property as security for a debt.
مانع (manih): An impediment that prevents the effectiveness of a contract, such as the mortgagee's lien.
مقتضی (muqtada): The effective cause or operative reason that makes the contract valid, here the owner's right to dispose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the impediment (the mortgagee's right) is removed, and the effective cause (ownership and capacity) exists.
C) Because the property is no longer mortgaged after payment.
D) Because the buyer gives permission.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, repayment of the debt extinguishes the mortgagee's lien, allowing the owner's inherent authority to sell to become effective — no additional consent is needed.

Transfer of Mortgagee's Right to the Price with Permission
Ruling on transfer of mortgagee's right to substitute upon permission of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mortgagee's right transfers to the price upon permitted sale and explain the qiyas from the debtor slave case.

Core Idea
A mortgagee's right is not lost upon sale with consent — it attaches to the price.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a critical nuance in Islamic mortgage (rahn). When the mortgagee permits the sale of the mortgaged property, does his right vanish? The Hanafi school rules that it survives, transferring to the price. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, centered on the concept that the right attaches to monetary value, not the specific object, and is reinforced by a powerful analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its legal effect
The mortgagee's right transfers to the price (badal) when he permits the sale. The ruling declares this transfer valid (sahih).
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The right is attached to monetary value (maliyyah), not the physical 'ayn. Therefore, when the property is replaced by its price, the right moves to the price.
The substitute (badal) takes the same ruling as the original (mubdal).
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The case of a debtor slave (asl) sold with creditors' consent: their right transfers to the price because they consent to transfer, not forfeiture. The illa (effective cause) is that the right is on the value.
This qiyas is jaliyyah and is cited as the proof.
4
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Recognize that the price becomes the new mortgaged property; the mortgage continues on the price.
The same principle applies to any similar exchange where the mortgagee consents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرتهن بیع کی اجازت دیدے تو مرتہن کا حق مرہون کے بدل کی جانب منتقل ہو جائے گا
واذا نفذ البيع باجازة المرتهن ينتقل حقه الى بدله هو الصحيح لان حقه تعلق بالمالية والبدل له حكم المبدل فصار كالعبد المديون اذا بيع برضاء الغرماء ينتقل حقهم الى البدل لانهم رضوا بالانتقال دون السقوط رأسا فكذا هذا
ترجمہ اور جبکہ بیع مرتہن کی اجازت سے نافذ ہوگئی تو مرتین کا حق مرہون کے بدل کی جانب منتقل ہو جائے گا یہی صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ مرتین کا حق مالیت کے ساتھ متعلق ہے اور بدل کے لئے مبدل کا حکم ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے غلام مدیون جبکہ غرماء کی رضا مندی سے بیچا جائے تو ان کا حق بدل کی جانب منتقل ہو جائے گا اس لئے کہ وہ (غرماء ) انتقال سے راضی ہوتے ہیں نہ کہ بالکلیہ سقوط سے پس ایسے ہی یہی ہے۔
تشریح مرتہن کی اجازت سے جب بیع نافذ ہوگئی تو مرہون کی جگہ شمن کو رہن رکھا جائے گا کیونکہ مرتہن کا حق عین رہن میں مالیت کے اعتبار سے متعلق تھا اور جیسے مبدل ( مرہون ) مال تھا ایسے ہی بدل ( شمن ) بھی مال ہے۔
خلاصہ کلام مرتہن کا حق بالکلیہ ساقط نہ ہوگا بلکہ اس کا انتقال ہو گا۔ اور اس کی مثال بالکل ایسی ہے جیسے عبد مدیون کو مالک نے قرض خواہوں کی اجازت سے بیچ دیا تو بیع جائز ہو گئی لیکن قرض خواہوں کا حق ساقط نہ ہوگا بلکہ منتقل ہو گا یعنی پہلے سے ان کا حق غلام کے ساتھ وابستہ تھا اب اس کے شمن کے ساتھ وابستہ ہو جائے گا ایسے ہی یہ بھی ہے۔
If the mortgagee permits the sale, then the right of the mortgagee will transfer to the substitute of the mortgaged property. And when the sale is executed with the permission of the mortgagee, his right transfers to its substitute; this is correct because his right is attached to the monetary value, and the substitute has the same ruling as the original. Thus it becomes like the case of a slave who is in debt: if he is sold with the consent of the creditors, their right transfers to the substitute because they consent to transfer, not to complete forfeiture. Similarly here. Clarification: When the sale is executed with the mortgagee's permission, the price will be held in pawn in place of the mortgaged property because the mortgagee's right was attached to the actual thing in terms of value. Just as the original (mortgaged property) was property, so too the substitute (price) is property. Summary: The mortgagee's right is not forfeited completely; rather it is transferred. And its example is exactly like the case of a debtor slave: if the owner sells him with the consent of the creditors, the sale is valid, but the creditors' right is not forfeited; it transfers, i.e., originally their right was attached to the slave, now it becomes attached to his price. Similarly here.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its commentary from Al-Hidayah. It states that if the mortgagee gives permission for the sale of the mortgaged property, his right is not extinguished but transfers to the price (thaman). The proof is that the mortgagee's right is attached to the monetary value (maliyyah) of the property, not the specific physical object. Therefore, when the property is sold with consent, the substitute (price) takes the place of the original (badal has hukm al-mubdal). The commentator draws an analogy (qiyas) with a debtor slave: if a slave is in debt and the owner sells him with the creditors' permission, the creditors' right transfers to the price. This analogy is used to illustrate that the mortgagee's right survives the sale and attaches to the price. The practical effect is that the price becomes subject to the mortgage (rahn) in place of the original property.

Key Terms
بدل (badal): The substitute (price or other property) that takes the place of the original mortgaged property.
مالیت (maliyyah): Monetary value or financial worth, as opposed to the physical entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The right remains on the property even after sale.
D) The right becomes void if the buyer objects.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's right survives sale with permission by transferring to the price, based on the principle that the right attaches to value, not the specific object, supported by qiyas on a debtor slave.

Mortgagee's Rescission of Unauthorized Sale: Two Hanafi Narrations
Two narrations on mortgagee's right to rescind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi narrations regarding the mortgagee's right to rescind an unauthorized sale of mortgaged property and identify which narration is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The mortgagee cannot rescind an unauthorized sale; the buyer may wait or seek judicial rescission.

When a mortgagor sells mortgaged property without the mortgagee's permission, the sale is initially suspended. A dispute arises: can the mortgagee rescind the sale himself? Two narrations from the Hanafi school provide different answers. This lesson examines both proofs and determines the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal issue
The mortgagor sells the mortgaged property without the mortgagee's consent. The sale is mawquf (suspended). The question is whether the mortgagee has the right to rescind (faskh) the sale directly.
2
Examine the first narration (Ibn Sa'id)
The first narration holds that the mortgagee can rescind because his right is akin to ownership. Once rescinded, the sale is void, and even if the mortgagor later redeems, the buyer has no claim.
This narration is weaker.
3
Examine the second, more correct narration (asah al-riwayatayn)
The second narration denies the mortgagee's right to rescind. The sale remains suspended. The buyer may either wait for redemption or petition the judge to rescind due to inability to deliver free title.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Understand the reasoning behind the correct position
The mortgagee's right of detention is not nullified by the sale; he only needs to protect his right. The authority to rescind lies with the judge, not the mortgagee. An analogy is drawn with a runaway slave before delivery.
The judge rescinds based on loss of ability to deliver.
5
Apply the ruling to a real scenario
Take a concrete case: A mortgages his house to B. Without B's permission, A sells the house to C. B objects and wants to cancel. According to the correct view, B cannot cancel; C can either wait for A to pay B or go to court to have the sale rescinded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
راہن نے دین ادا نہیں کیا اور مرتہن نے بیع کی اجازت نہیں دیتی تو بیع نہ ہوگی
وان لم يجز المرتهن البيع وفسخه انفسخ في رواية حتى لو افتك الراهن الرهن لا سبيل للمشترى عليه لان الحق الثابت للمرتهن بمنزلة الملك فصار كالمالک له ان يجيزوله ان يفسخ
ترجمہ اور اگر مرتہن نے اجازت نہ دی اور اس کو فسخ کر دیا تو ایک روایت کے مطابق فسخ ہو گئی یہاں تک کہ اگر راہن نے رہن کو چھڑا لیا تو رہن پر مشتری کے لئے کوئی سبیل نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ وہ حق جو مرتہن کے لئے ثابت ہے وہ ملک کے درجہ میں ہے تو مرتہن مالک کے مثل ہو گیا جس کو حق ہے کہ اجازت دے اور حق ہے کہ فسخ کر دے۔
تشریح اگر راہن نے مرتہن کا قرض ادا نہ کیا ہو اور نہ مرتہن نے بیع کی اجازت دی تو بیع نہ ہوگی (کما مر) اب سوال یہ ہے کہ اگر مرتہن نے اس بیع کو فسخ کر دیا تو بیع فسخ ہوگی یا نہیں تو ابن ساعد کی روایت یہ ہے کہ بیع فسخ ہو جائے گی اور جب بیع فسخ ہوگئی تو اب مشتری کا کوئی بیع میں حصہ نہ رہا لہذا اگر اس کے بعد راہن مرتہن کا قرض ادا کر دے اور مرہون کو اس کے پاس سے چھڑالے تو اب مشتری کو یہ حق نہ ہوگا کہ راہن سے مبیع کو لے لے کیونکہ بیع پہلے ہی ختم ہو چکی ہے۔
سوال ... مرتہن کے فسخ کرنے سے بیع کیوں فسخ ہو جائے گی جبکہ مرتہن اس کا مالک نہیں ہے؟
جواب ... اگر چہ وہ مالک تو نہیں مگر مالک کے مثل ہے اور مالک کو حق ہوتا ہے کہ خواہ عقد کو فسخ کر دے اور خواہ اس کی اجازت دیدے. سوال.. مرتہن مالک کے درجہ میں کیسے ہے؟
جواب ... اس لئے کہ اس میں ملکیت کی علامت پائی جاتی ہے جیسے راہن کا تصرف ممنوع ہونا اور اگر راہن اس سے وطی کرے تو عقد کا ضامن ہونا وغیرہ۔
اصح ہے۔
وفى اصح الراويتين لا ينفسخ بفسخه لانه لوثبت حق الفسخ له انما يثبت ضرورة صيانة حقه وحقه في الحبس لا يبطل بانعقاد هذا العقد فبقى موقوفا فان شا المشترى صبر حتى يفتك الراهن الرهن اذا العجز على شرف الزوال وان شاء رفع الامر الى القاضى وللقاضى ان يفسخ لفوات القدرة على التسليم ولاية الفسخ الى القاضى لا اليه وصار كما اذا ابق العبد المشترى قبل القبض فانه يتخير المشترى لما ذكرنا كذالك هذا
ترجمہ اور دوروایتوں میں سے اصح روایت کے مطابق مرتہن کے فسخ کرنے سے بیع فسخ نہ ہوگی اس لئے کہ اگر مرتہن کے لئے حق فسخ ثابت ہوگا تو اپنے حق کی حفاظت کی ضرورت سے ثابت ہوگا اور اس عقد کے معتقد ہونے سے مرتہن کے جس کا حق باطل نہ ہوگا تو عقد موقوف باقی رہا۔ پس اگر مشتری چاہے تو صبر کرے یہاں تک کے راہن رہن کو چھڑا لے اس لئے کہ عاجزوی زوال کے کنارے پر ہے اور اگر چاہے تو اس معاملہ کا مرافعہ قاضی کی طرف کرے اور قاضی کو حق ہے کہ بیع فسخ کر دے تسلیم پر قدرت کے فوت ہونے کی وجہ سے اور فسخ کی ولایت قاضی کی طرف ہے نہ کہ مرتہن کی طرف اور ایسا ہو گیا جیسے خریدا ہوا غلام جبکہ وہ قبضہ سے پہلے بھاگ جائے تو مشتری مختار ہوتا ہے اسی دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے ذکر کر دی ہے ایسی ہی یہ ۔
تشریح یہ دوسری روایت ہے جو اصح ہے کہ مرتہن کے فسخ کرنے سے بھی بیع فسخ نہ ہوگی بلکہ موقوف رہے گی تو اب مشتری کو اختیار ہے اگر چاہے صبر کرے اور جب راہن مرتہن کا قرض ادا کر کے مرہون کو چھڑالے اس وقت مبیع کو راہن سے لے لے اور اگر چاہے تو اس معاملہ کو
If the mortgagor has not paid the debt and the mortgagee does not permit the sale, the sale does not occur. And if the mortgagee did not permit the sale and rescinds it, in one narration it is rescinded, so much so that if the mortgagor redeems the pawn, the buyer has no recourse to the mortgaged property, because the right established for the mortgagee is like ownership, so he becomes like the owner, having the right to permit or rescind. ... And in the more correct of the two narrations, the sale is not rescinded by his rescission, because if the right of rescission were established for him, it would be established only out of necessity to protect his right, and his right of detention is not nullified by the conclusion of this contract, so it remains suspended. Then if the buyer wishes, he may wait until the mortgagor redeems the pawn, because the inability is on the verge of removal; and if he wishes, he may refer the matter to the judge, and the judge has the right to rescind due to the loss of ability to deliver. The authority of rescission belongs to the judge, not to him (the mortgagee). This is like the case of a purchased slave who runs away before delivery: the buyer has a choice, for the reason we mentioned. Similarly here.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the mortgagee's ability to rescind a sale of the mortgaged property that he did not authorize. Two narrations are presented. The first narration (from Ibn Sam'ah? The text says 'Ibn Sa'id') holds that the mortgagee can rescind the sale because his right is akin to ownership, and he has the authority to permit or void the contract. The second narration, which is considered more correct (asah), denies the mortgagee the right to rescind. The reasoning is that the mortgagee's right of detention (habs) is not invalidated by the sale; rather, the sale remains suspended (mawquf). The mortgagee's interest is protected by the fact that he retains possession. The buyer is given the option to either wait for the mortgagor to repay and free the property, or to bring the case before a judge, who can rescind the sale due to the seller's inability to deliver clear title. The authority to rescind lies with the judge, not the mortgagee. An analogy is drawn with a purchased slave who runs away before delivery: the buyer may wait or cancel.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the sale contract, rendering it void.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; the contract is not immediately effective but awaits removal of impediment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the more correct narration (اصح الروایتین) in the Hanafi school, what happens when the mortgagee rescinds a sale that he did not permit?
A) The sale is immediately rescinded and void.
B) The sale remains suspended, and the buyer may either wait for the mortgagor to pay or seek judicial rescission.
C) The mortgagee has the final say and can cancel the sale at any time.
D) The sale is valid despite the mortgagee's objection.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a mortgagee cannot unilaterally rescind an unauthorized sale; the sale remains suspended and only a judge may rescind it.

Mortgagee's Cancellation: Does It Annul the Sale?
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on faskh by mortgagee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on whether the mortgagee's faskh annuls a sale of mortgaged property, and apply the rule that only a judge can annul due to inability to deliver.

Core Idea
The mortgagee's cancellation does not annul the sale; only a judge can annul for inability to deliver.

In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, we examine a key intra-Hanafi dispute: can a mortgagee (murtahin) unilaterally cancel a sale of the mortgaged property made by the mortgagor (rahin) without permission? The text presents two narrations within the school. The relied-upon view, marked as 'asahh', holds that the sale survives the mortgagee's cancellation and remains suspended, giving the buyer the option to wait or seek judicial annulment. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the analogy to a runaway slave, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the relied-upon Hanafi position
Identify the asahh al-riwayatayn: the mortgagee's faskh does not annul the sale; it remains mawquf (suspended). The mortgagee's right of retention (haqq al-habs) is not invalidated.
2
Identify the opposing narration
Ibn Sa'd's narration holds that the mortgagee's faskh annuls the sale immediately, because the mortgagee is like an owner.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
The opposing argument: the mortgagee, though not owner, has signs of ownership (restriction on mortgagor's disposition, liability for intercourse). Thus, he should have the right to annul.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The relied-upon view counters: the mortgagee's right is only retention, not ownership. The authority to annul belongs solely to the judge, because only a judge can determine that the mortgagor is unable to deliver.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the opposing narration, the buyer loses all claim immediately. Under the relied-upon view, the buyer has two options: wait for the mortgagor to redeem, or ask the judge to annul and recover the price.

Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, volume 14. He became like one who has the right to permit and the right to cancel. Chapter of Mortgage. Explanation: If the mortgagor has not paid the debt of the mortgagee and the mortgagee has not given permission for the sale, then the sale will not take place. Now the question is: if the mortgagee cancels this sale, will the sale be annulled or not? According to the narration of Ibn Sa'd, the sale will be annulled, and when the sale is annulled, the buyer has no share in the sale. Therefore, if after that the mortgagor pays the debt of the mortgagee and redeems the mortgaged property from him, the buyer will not have the right to take the sold property from the mortgagor because the sale has already ended. Question: Why would the sale be annulled by the mortgagee's cancellation when the mortgagee is not its owner? Answer: Although he is not the owner, he is like the owner, and the owner has the right to either annul the contract or permit it. Question: How is the mortgagee in the position of an owner? Answer: Because signs of ownership are found in him, such as the mortgagor's disposition being restricted and if the mortgagor has intercourse with her, he becomes liable for the contract, etc. The more correct is the other narration. Regarding cancellation, there are two narrations: one mentioned above, and the other narration is that the sale will not be annulled, and the second narration is more correct. Whether the sale is annulled by the mortgagee's cancellation or not. According to the more correct of the two narrations, it is not annulled by his cancellation, because if the right of cancellation was established for him, it would only be established out of necessity to protect his right, and his right of retention is not invalidated by the conclusion of this contract, so it remains suspended. So if the buyer wishes, he may wait until the mortgagor redeems the mortgage because the inability is on the verge of vanishing, and if he wishes, he may refer the matter to the judge, and the judge has the right to annul due to loss of ability to deliver. The authority of annulment belongs to the judge, not to him. And it is like the case of the purchased slave who runs away before delivery; then the buyer has a choice, for the reason we mentioned. So this is similar. Explanation: This second narration, which is more correct, is that the sale will not be annulled by the mortgagee's cancellation, but will remain suspended. So the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he may wait, and when the mortgagor pays the mortgagee's debt and redeems the mortgaged property, then he may take the sold property from the mortgagor; and if he wishes, he may take the matter to the court of the judge so that he may annul the contract, because the mortgagor does not have the ability to deliver. In any case, the authority of annulment belongs to the judge, not to the mortgagee. Question: Is there any harm to the mortgagee in the conclusion of the sale? Answer: There is no harm, because he had the right of retention, and it will remain so now, and it is for this reason that because the mortgaged property is held by the mortgagee, the sale is considered suspended and the buyer is given a choice. Question: Why is the buyer given the option to wait? Answer: Because now the mortgagor is unable to deliver the sale, but in the future he may become able by paying the debt, so as long as the buyer waits, the author said: 'because the inability is on the verge of vanishing.' Question: Can you give an example of this? Answer: Yes! If a buyer purchases a slave, but before delivery the slave runs away, then here too the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he may wait until the slave returns, and if he wishes, he may take the case to the judge's court, and he will annul it.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage addresses a key issue in the Hanafi law of mortgage (rahn): whether the mortgagee (murtahin) can cancel (faskh) a sale of the mortgaged property made by the mortgagor (rahin) without his permission. The text presents two narrations within the Hanafi school: one (attributed to Ibn Sa'd) holds that the mortgagee's cancellation annuls the sale, while the relied-upon (asahh) position states that the sale is not annulled but remains suspended (mawquf). The reasoning is that the mortgagee's right of retention (haqq al-habs) is not invalidated by the sale, so the contract remains valid but ineffective pending permission. The buyer is given two options: wait for the mortgagor to redeem the property, or seek judicial annulment by the judge due to the mortgagor's inability to deliver. The text analogizes this to a purchased slave who runs away before delivery, where the buyer similarly has a choice. This ruling highlights the principle that the mortgagee's authority does not extend to unilaterally cancelling a third-party contract; only a judge can annul for valid cause.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Cancellation or annulment of a contract; here, the mortgagee's act of cancelling the sale.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended contract; the sale is valid but its effect depends on a condition (here, mortgagee's permission or judge's annulment).
حق حبس (haqq al-habs): Right of retention; the mortgagee's right to hold the mortgaged property until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi ruling (asahh al-riwayatayn), what happens when a mortgagee cancels a sale made by the mortgagor without permission?
A) The sale is annulled immediately.
B) The sale remains suspended and only a judge can annul it.
C) The buyer must return the property.
D) The mortgagee must pay damages.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's cancellation does not annul the sale of mortgaged property; the sale remains suspended, and the buyer may wait for redemption or seek judicial annulment.

Sequential Sales of Mortgaged Property Suspended
Ruling on second sale being suspended when first is also suspended

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the status of a second sale of mortgaged property when the first sale is also suspended, and explain why the second remains suspended.

Core Idea
A suspended contract does not prevent another suspension – both sales hang on the mortgagee's permission.

This passage examines a scenario where a mortgagor sells the same mortgaged property to two different buyers without the mortgagee's permission. The key question: does the first suspended sale block the second from being valid? The Hanafi answer is clear: the second is also suspended, not void, because a suspended contract does not preclude another similar suspension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The mortgagor sells the same property twice. Both sales are ruled as suspended (mawquf) on the mortgagee's permission.
2
Understand the legal premise
A suspended contract is not yet effective, so it does not prevent another contract from being similarly suspended.
This is key: mawquf ≠ batil.
3
Apply the rule
The second sale is also suspended. The mortgagee may permit either sale; whichever is permitted becomes effective.
4
Note the implication
If the mortgagee permits the second sale, it becomes valid. Permitting the first would also make it valid a fortiori.
The author specifically mentions the second to show it can be permitted despite the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ اور اگر مرہون کو راہن نے کسی شخص کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا پھر اس کو دوبارہ دوسرے شخص کے ہاتھ بیچ دیا مرتہن کی اجازت سے پہلے تو دوسری بیع مرتہن کی اجازت پر موقوف ہے اس لئے کہ پہلی بیع نافذ نہیں ہوئی اور موقوف دوسری کے توقف کو نہیں روکتا پس اگر مرتہن نے دوسری بیع کی اجازت دیدی تو دوسری بیع جائز ہے۔
تشریح مرتہن کی اجازت کے بغیر اگر راہن مرہون کو فروخت کرے تو بیع موقوف ہوتی ہے ( کمامر ) اور ابھی مرتہن نے اجازت نہیں دی تھی کہ راہن نے دوسرے شخص کے ہاتھ پھر مرہون کو اگر فروخت کر دیا تو اب کیا ہوگا ؟ تو فرمایا کہ جیسے اول موقوف تھی ایسے ہی ثانی بھی موقوف ہو گی اب مرتہن اگر بیع اول کی اجازت دے تو بیع اول نافذ ہوگی اور اگر بیع ثانی کی اجازت دے تو بیع ثانی نافذ ہوگی ۔
سوال مصنف نے تو صرف یہ ذکر فرمایا ہے کہ اگر بیع ثانی کی اجازت دیدی تو وہی جائز ہوگی؟ جواب ... جی ہاں ! لیکن جب ثانی جائز ہوگئی تو اگر اول کی اجازت دیدے تو وہ بدرجہ اولی جائز ہو جائے گی۔ سوال پھر مصنف نے اس ثانی کو کیوں خاص کر دیا ؟
And if the mortgagor sells it to a man, then sells it a second time to another before the mortgagee permits it, the second is also suspended on his permission, because the first did not take effect, and a suspended contract does not prevent the suspension of the second. So if the mortgagee permits the second sale, the second becomes valid. Translation: And if the mortgagor sells the mortgaged property to one person, then sells it again to a second person before the mortgagee's permission, then the second sale is also suspended on the mortgagee's permission, because the first sale did not take effect, and a suspended contract does not prevent the suspension of the second. So if the mortgagee permits the second sale, the second sale is valid. Explanation: Without the mortgagee's permission, if the mortgagor sells the mortgaged property, the sale is suspended. And before the mortgagee gave permission, the mortgagor sold the mortgaged property to another person. So what happens? It is said that just as the first was suspended, similarly the second will also be suspended. Now if the mortgagee permits the first sale, the first sale becomes effective; and if he permits the second sale, the second sale becomes effective. Question: The author only mentioned that if he permits the second sale, then that one becomes valid? Answer: Yes! But when the second becomes valid, if he permits the first, it becomes valid a fortiori. Question: Then why did the author specifically mention the second?
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where the mortgagor (rahin) sells the same mortgaged property to two different buyers sequentially, without the mortgagee's (murtahin) permission for either sale. The ruling is that both sales are suspended (mawquf) on the mortgagee's permission. The first sale is suspended because it lacks permission; the second sale is also suspended because the first is not effective and a suspended contract does not prevent another contract from being similarly suspended. This follows from the general Hanafi principle that a suspended contract (mawquf) is not considered fully concluded. The mortgagee retains the right to permit either sale; whichever he permits becomes effective and binding. The text notes that if he permits the second, it becomes valid, and by implication, permitting the first would also make it valid a fortiori. The reason the author explicitly mentions the second sale is to emphasize that the possibility of permission for the second exists even in the presence of the first contract.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended contract; a sale whose effectiveness depends on a third party's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mortgagor sells the same mortgaged property to two different buyers without the mortgagee's permission, what is the status of the second sale?
A) It is void.
B) It is suspended on the mortgagee's permission, like the first.
C) It is valid only if the first buyer agrees.
D) It takes priority over the first.

Key Takeaway
A suspended contract does not prevent another contract from being similarly suspended; both sales of mortgaged property hang on the mortgagee's permission.

Unauthorized Multiple Transactions by Mortgagor: Sale vs. Other Contracts
Expands ruling on multiple transactions after unauthorized sale of pawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on a second sale from other contracts (lease, gift, repledge) after an unauthorized sale of a pawn, and state the legal reasoning based on the pledgee's right to the substitute.

Core Idea
A second sale can be validated by the pledgee's permission; other contracts remove the impediment to the first sale because the pledgee has no right in their substitutes.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a mortgagor's unauthorized sale of the pawn is suspended upon the pledgee's permission. Here, the commentator addresses what happens if the mortgagor enters into further transactions after that first sale but before permission. The ruling distinguishes between a second sale and other contracts like lease, gift, or repledge. The key is the nature of the pledgee's right: it attaches to the substitute (badal) in a sale but not in other contracts. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two scenarios after an unauthorized first sale: (1) a second sale, and (2) a lease, gift, or repledge. Understand the difference in how the pledgee's permission affects each.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For a second sale, the pledgee can permit either sale, and the permitted one becomes valid. For lease/gift/repledge, if the pledgee permits them, the first sale (not the new contract) becomes valid.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The pledgee's right is in the monetary value of the corpus (ayn). In a sale, his right attaches to the price (badal), so he has a beneficial interest in which sale takes effect. In lease/gift/repledge, there is no badal for the corpus: gift and repledge have no substitute, and lease has a substitute for usufruct only. Thus, his permission for these contracts amounts to relinquishing his right, removing the impediment to the first sale.
4
Apply to the lease case
If the mortgagor sells first, then leases, and the pledgee permits the lease, the lease does not become valid. Instead, the first sale becomes valid because the pledgee's permission removes his objection to the sale, and the lease has no effect on his right.
5
Recognise the practical consequence
In a double sale, the pledgee has a choice which sale to validate; but in a sale followed by other contracts, the pledgee's permission for those contracts automatically validates the first sale, leaving the other contract invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرتبہن کی اجازت کے بغیر راہن مرہون کو فروخت کر دے تو بیع موقوف ہوگی۔ ولو باعه الراهن من رجل ثم باعه بيعا ثانيا من غيره قبل ان يجيزه المرتهن فالثانى موقوف ايضا على اجازته لان الاول لم ينفذ والموقوف لايمنع توقف الثانى فلو اجاز المرتهن البيع الثانى جاز الثانى. ترجمہ: اور اگر مرہون کو راہن نے کسی شخص کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا پھر اس کو دوبارہ دوسرے شخص کے ہاتھ بیچ دیا مرتبہن کی اجازت سے پہلے تو دوسری بیع مرتہن کی اجازت پر موقوف ہے اس لئے کہ پہلی بیع نافذ نہیں ہوئی اور موقوف دوسری کے توقف کو نہیں روکتا پس اگر مرتہن نے دوسری بیع کی اجازت دیدی تو دوسری بیع جائز ہے۔ تشریح: مرتہن کی اجازت کے بغیر اگر راہن مرہون کو فروخت کرے تو بیع موقوف ہوتی ہے (کما مر) اور ابھی مرتہن نے اجازت نہیں دی تھی کہ راہن نے دوسرے شخص کے ہاتھ پھر مرہون کو اگر فروخت کر دیا تو اب کیا ہوگا؟ تو فرمایا کہ جیسے اول موقوف تھی ایسے ہی ثانی بھی موقوف ہو گی اب مرتہن اگر بیع اول کی اجازت دے تو بیع اول نافذ ہوگی اور اگر بیع ثانی کی اجازت دے تو بیع ثانی نافذ ہوگی۔ سوال مصنف نے تو صرف یہ ذکر فرمایا ہے کہ اگر بیع ثانی کی اجازت دیدی تو وہی جائز ہوگی؟ جواب جی ہاں ! لیکن جب ثانی جائز ہوگئی تو اگر اول کی اجازت دیدے تو وہ بدرجہ اولی جائز ہو جائے گی۔ سوال پھر مصنف نے اس ثانی کو کیوں خاص کر دیا ؟ جواب بیع ثانی اور ان دوسرے عقود کے درمیان فرق کرنے کی وجہ سے جن کا ذکر ابھی آ رہا ہے۔ سنئے۔ ولو باع الراهن ثم اجر اووهب اورهن من غيره واجاز المرتهن لهذه العقود جاز البيع الاول. ترجمہ: اور راہن نے بیع کی پھر اجارہ پر دیا یا ہبہ کر دیا یا اس کے غیر کے ہاتھ رہن دیدیا اور مرتہن نے ان عقود کی اجازت دیدی تو بیع اول جائز ہے۔ تشریح: یعنی اگر بیع کے بعد دوبارہ بیع کی تو مرتہن جونسی بیع کی اجازت دیدے وہی جائز ہو جائے گی اور اگر بیع کے بعد اجارہ پر دیدے یا بیع کے بعد کسی کو ہبہ کر دے یا بیع کے بعد کسی دوسرے کے پاس رہن رکھ دے اور اب مرتہن اجارہ یا ہبہ یا رہن کی اجازت دیدے تب بھی یہ عقود ثلاثہ جائز نہ ہوں گے بلکہ ان سے پہلے جو بیع کی گئی تھی صرف وہ جائز ہو گی۔ سوال اس کو بیع اول کیوں کہا گیا ہے جبکہ دو بیع یہاں نہیں ہیں؟ جواب اس کو بیع اول اس لئے کہہ دیا کہ باقی عقود بیع کے بعد میں تو بیع ان سے پہلے ہو گئی۔ سوال اس کی کیا وجہ ہے کہ دو بیع کی صورت میں تو وہ بیع جائز ہوتی ہے جس کی مرتہن اجازت دے اور دیگر عقود کی اجازت کے باوجود بھی بیع اول ہی نافذ ہوتی ہے؟ جواب آئندہ عبارت میں مصنف اس کا جواب دے رہے ہیں۔ والفرق ان المرتهن ذو حظ من البيع الثانى لانه يتعلق حقه ببدله فيصح تعيينه لتعلق فائدته به اما لاحق له في هذه العقود لانه لا بدل فى الهبة والرهن والذى فى الاجارة بدل المنفعة لا بدل العين وحقه فى مالية العين لا فى المنفعة فكانت إجارته اسقاطا لحقه فزال المانع فنفذ البيع الأول فوضح الفرق. ترجمہ: اور فرق یہ ہے کہ مرتہن حصہ والا ہے دوسری بیع سے اس لئے کہ اس کا حق مرہون کے شمن کے ساتھ متعلق ہے تو مرتہن کی تعیین صحیح ہے مرتہن کے فائدہ کے متعلق ہونے کی وجہ سے ثانی کے ساتھ بہر حال ان عقود ثلاثہ میں اس کا کوئی حق نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ ہبہ اور رہن میں تو کوئی بدل ہی نہیں ہے اور جو بدل اجارہ میں ہے وہ منفعت کا بدل ہے عین کا بدل نہیں ہے اور مرتہن کا حق عین کی مالیت میں ہے نہ کہ منفعت میں تو مرتہن کی اجازت اپنے حق کا اسقاط ہے تو مانع زائل ہو گیا پس بیع اول نافذ ہو جائے گی پس فرق واضح ہو گیا۔
If the mortgagor sells the pawn to a man, then sells it a second time to another before the pledgee permits it, the second sale is also suspended upon his permission, because the first did not take effect, and a suspended contract does not prevent the suspension of the second. So if the pledgee permits the second sale, the second becomes valid. And if the mortgagor sells, then leases, or gives as a gift, or repledges to another, and the pledgee permits these contracts, the first sale becomes valid. The difference is that the pledgee has a share in the second sale because his right attaches to its substitute, so his designation is valid due to his benefit being attached to it. But he has no right in these three contracts because there is no substitute in gift and pledge, and what is in lease is a substitute for usufruct, not for the corpus. His right is in the monetary value of the corpus, not in the usufruct. So his permission is a relinquishment of his right, thus the impediment is removed and the first sale takes effect. So the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This is a ruling expanded by the commentator (Mufti Muhammad Sharif al-Husayni) on Al-Hidayah. The passage addresses the scenario where a mortgagor sells the pawn without the pledgee's permission, and then either sells it again or enters into another type of contract (lease, gift, repledge) before the pledgee permits. The key ruling: if the second transaction is another sale, the pledgee can choose which sale to permit, and that one becomes valid. If the second transaction is a lease, gift, or repledge, then the pledgee's permission for those does not make them valid; instead, the first sale (which was made before these contracts) becomes valid. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that in a sale, the pledgee's right is attached to the price (badal), so he has a stake in which sale goes through, allowing him to choose. In other contracts, there is no substitute for the corpus: gift and repledge have no substitute, and lease has a substitute for usufruct only, not the corpus. Since the pledgee's right is in the corpus' value, his permission for these contracts effectively relinquishes his right, removing the impediment to the first sale. The author emphasizes that the distinction (farq) is clear: the pledgee has a beneficial interest (hazz) only in a second sale, not in the other contracts.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawquf): A contract that is suspended upon the permission of a third party (here the pledgee) for its validity.
بدل (badal): The substitute or consideration given in exchange for the pawn (i.e., the sale price).
عین (ayn): The physical corpus of the pawned property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The lease becomes valid.
B) The original sale becomes valid.
C) Both the sale and lease are invalid.
D) The pledgee must choose which to validate.

Key Takeaway
When a mortgagor makes multiple transactions after an unauthorized sale, the pledgee's permission validates a second sale directly, but for other contracts it validates the first sale because the pledgee has no right in their substitutes.

Why Mortgagee's Permission Validates a Prior Sale but Not Later Contracts
Explaining why mortgagee's permission validates a prior sale but not other contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between sale and other contracts regarding the mortgagee's permission and apply it to determine which disposition becomes effective.

Core Idea
A mortgagee's permission for a later sale directs his right to its price; for other contracts, his permission waives his right, removing the prior sale's impediment.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a pledgor sells a pledged item, the sale is suspended (mawquf) pending the mortgagee's permission. But if the pledgor later leases, gifts, or re-pledges, and the mortgagee permits these, the original sale becomes immediately effective. This lesson unpacks the rational distinction: the mortgagee has a stake in the second sale because his right transfers to the price, whereas in lease, gift, and re-pledge, he has no such stake, so his permission is a waiver that removes the obstacle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principal scenario
The pledgor sells the pledged item, then later performs another contract (lease, gift, re-pledge). The mortgagee then permits the later contract. Determine which contract becomes effective.
2
Trace the mortgagee's interest in the second sale
Why does the mortgagee have a share (hazz) in the second sale? Because his security right attaches to the substitute (badal) – the price – which becomes the new subject of his lien.
In a sale, the substitute is for the substance ('ayn).
3
Analyze why no share in lease, gift, or re-pledge
Lease's substitute is for usufruct, not the substance; gift and re-pledge have no substitute at all. The mortgagee's right is only in the monetary value of the substance ('ayn). So these contracts do not involve his interest.
Mudda' al-haqq: haqq al-murtahin fi maliyyat al-'ayn la fi al-manfa'ah.
4
Understand the consequence of permission
If the mortgagee permits a contract in which he has no stake, his permission is effectively a relinquishment (isqat) of his right, thereby removing the impediment that previously suspended the first sale.
The permission is not a direct validation of the later contract.
5
Apply the ruling
The first sale becomes nafidh (effective), and the later contract (lease, gift, re-pledge) is valid between the parties but subject to the mortgagee's prior right being transferred to the sale proceeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو باع الراهن ثم أجر أو وهب أو رهن من غيره وأجاز المرتهن لهذه العقود جاز البيع الأول والفرق أن المرتهن ذو حظ من البيع الثاني لأنه يتعلق حقه ببدله فيصح تعيينه لتعلق فائدته به وأما لا حق له في هذه العقود لأنه لا بدل في الهبة والرهن والذي في الإجارة بدل المنفعة لا بدل العين وحقه في مالية العين لا في المنفعة فكانت إجارته إسقاطا لحقه فزال المانع فنفذ البيع الأول فوضح الفرق
If the pledgor sells, then leases, gives as gift, or pledges to someone else, and the mortgagee permits these contracts, then the first sale is valid. The difference is that the mortgagee has a share in the second sale because his right attaches to its substitute, so his designation is valid due to his benefit attaching to it. But he has no right in these contracts because there is no substitute in gift and pledge, and what is in lease is a substitute for usufruct, not for the substance, and his right is in the monetary value of the substance, not in the usufruct. So his permission is a relinquishment of his right, thus the impediment is removed, and the first sale becomes effective. So the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the effect of the mortgagee's permission on multiple dispositions by the pledgor. The ruling states that if the pledgor sells a pledged item, then later leases it, gives it as a gift, or pledges it to another, and the mortgagee permits these later contracts, the first sale becomes valid and effective. The proof lies in the distinction: the mortgagee has a beneficial interest in the second sale because his right transfers to its price (the substitute), so his permission specifically designates which sale he allows. However, in lease, gift, and pledge to another, the mortgagee has no stake: gift and pledge have no substitute at all, and lease's substitute (rent) is for usufruct, not for the substance (ayn) which is the subject of the pledge. Therefore, the mortgagee's permission for these contracts effectively relinquishes his right, removing the impediment to the first sale. Thus, the first sale is executed, not the later contracts. The commentator emphasizes that the mortgagee's permission in the second case is not a choice but a waiver, so the prior sale takes effect.

Key Terms
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee or pawnee who holds the pledged item as security.
بدل (badal): Substitute or price; in sale, it is the price (thaman); in lease, it is the rent (ujrah) for usufruct.
عين ('ayn): The physical substance or asset itself, as opposed to its usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if a pledgor sells a pledged item and then leases it, and the mortgagee permits the lease, which contract becomes effective?
A) The lease becomes effective.
B) The first sale becomes effective.
C) Both become effective.
D) Neither becomes effective.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's permission for a later non-sale contract waives his right, enabling the prior sale to become effective.

Manumission of a Pledged Slave: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling with Shafi'i dispute analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a pledgor's manumission of a pledged slave and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Manumission is effective because it is worship; the mortgagee's right shifts to value.

In this passage, al-Quduri states the Hanafi position that a pledgor's manumission of a pledged slave is immediately effective. He then contrasts it with the view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who in some sayings conditions effectiveness on the pledgor's solvency. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling as stated: the manumission is effective (nafadha). Note that no condition of wealth is attached.
This is the established view of the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Recognize that al-Shafi'i has multiple sayings; the text mentions one where it is ineffective if the pledgor is insolvent, and another where it is effective if wealthy.
The disagreement is about the effect of the pledgor's financial status.
3
Follow the Shafi'i analogy
The Shafi'i reasoning compares manumission to sale: both nullify the mortgagee's right. Since sale of a pledged item is suspended, so should be manumission. However, when the pledgor is wealthy, the right can be secured by a guarantee (tadmin), so it becomes effective.
This is an argument by qiyas (analogy).
4
Analyze the Hanafi distinction
The Hanafi school distinguishes manumission from sale because manumission is an act of worship (taqarrub) and thus stronger; the mortgagee's right is not completely nullified but shifts to the value of the slave.
The key difference: worship vs. mere contract.
5
Determine the practical consequence
According to the Hanafi school, the slave is free immediately. The mortgagee can claim the value from the pledgor. If the pledgor is insolvent, the slave may be required to work to pay off the debt under certain conditions.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: immediate freedom vs. conditional freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولو أعتق الراهن عبد الرهن نفذ عتقه وفي بعض أقوال الشافعي لا ينفذ إذا كان المعتق معسرا لأن في تنفيذه إبطال حق المرتهن فأشبه البيع بخلاف ما إذا كان موسرا حيث ينفذ على بعض أقواله لأنه لا يبطل حقه معنى بالتضمين وبخلاف إعتاق المستأجر لأن الإجارة تبقى مدتها إذا الحر يقبلها وأما الرهن فلا يبقى
He said: If the pledgor manumits the pledged slave, his manumission is effective. In some sayings of al-Shafi'i, it is not effective if the manumitter is insolvent, because in its execution there is nullification of the mortgagee's right, so it resembles sale. Contrary to the case when he is wealthy, for in some of his sayings it is effective because his right is not nullified in meaning due to the guarantee. And contrary to the manumission by a lessee, because the lease continues for its term since the free person accepts it, but the pledge does not remain.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on a pledgor manumitting a pledged slave, along with the Shafi'i disagreement. The Hanafi position, as stated by al-Quduri, is that the manumission is effective immediately, regardless of the pledgor's financial status. Imam al-Shafi'i has three sayings, two of which are mentioned: first, that it is absolutely ineffective; second, that it is effective if the pledgor is wealthy but not if insolvent. The reasoning for the Shafi'i view is that manumission, like sale, would nullify the mortgagee's right, so if sale is suspended, manumission should also be suspended. However, when the pledgor is wealthy, the mortgagee's right can be replaced by a guarantee (tadmin), so the nullification is not complete, hence manumission is effective. The Hanafi school distinguishes manumission from sale: manumission is a stronger act of worship (taqarrub) and takes precedence, so it is effective. The text also contrasts with the case of a musta'jir (lessee) freeing a leased slave: the lease contract's term continues because the freed slave can accept the lease, but a pledge contract does not continue because the pledged item cannot be used freely.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Manumission; the act of freeing a slave.
معسر (mu'sir): Insolvent; one who does not have wealth beyond basic needs.
تضمين (tadmin): Guarantee or indemnity; holding someone liable for the value of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a pledgor manumits a pledged slave while being insolvent?
A) The manumission is not effective because it nullifies the mortgagee's right.
B) The manumission is effective, but the slave must be returned to the mortgagee.
C) The manumission is effective, and the slave becomes free immediately.
D) The manumission is suspended until the pledgor becomes wealthy.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, manumission of a pledged slave by the pledgor is effective immediately, and the mortgagee's right is secured through the slave's value.

Freeing a Pledged Slave: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Positions
Explains the ruling on a pledgor freeing a pledged slave and the inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on freeing a pledged slave, explain the Hanafi proof using qiyas, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterarguments by comparing the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that a pledgor's freeing of a pledged slave is effective unconditionally because ownership overrides the temporary pledge encumbrance.

This lesson examines a classical inter-madhhab dispute: can a pledgor (debtor) free a slave he has given as collateral? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools disagree on the validity of such manumission without the pledgee's consent. We will analyze the Hanafi position, its proofs, and the Shafi'i objections.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
State clearly that the Hanafi school rules the freeing of a pledged slave as effective unconditionally, regardless of the pledgor's wealth or the pledgee's consent.
2
Identify the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i has three reported opinions: absolute effectiveness, absolute ineffectiveness, and effectiveness only if the pledgor is wealthy. The text focuses on the third opinion.
The author (Ashraf al-Hidaya) mentions the third opinion as the one discussed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Proof (Qiyas)
The Hanafi proof uses analogy: the pledgor owns the slave's corpus (raqabah), and lack of possession does not invalidate freeing, as seen in cases of freeing a purchased slave before delivery, a runaway, or a usurped slave.
The effective cause is that ownership of the corpus remains, and the pledge is a temporary hindrance.
4
Examine the Shafi'i Counterproof and Hanafi Response
The Shafi'i argue that freeing nullifies the pledgee's right, resembling sale which is suspended. The Hanafis respond that freeing differs from sale (it does not transfer ownership, and the pledgee's right is to value, not corpus) and note inconsistency with the lease case.
In lease, freeing is effective despite the lessee's right, because freedom and lease are compatible; pledge and freedom are incompatible.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi law, the slave is free immediately; in Shafi'i law, if the pledgor is insolvent, the freeing is invalid; if wealthy, it may be valid but the pledgee can claim compensation.
This affects the rights of the pledgee and the status of the slave.

Source Text

فلا يبقى
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور اگر راہن نے رہن کا غلام آزاد کر دیا تو اس کا عشق نافذ ہو جائے گا اور شافعی کے بعض اقوال میں نافذ نہ ہوگا جبکہ معتق تنگدست ہو اس لئے کہ حق کے نافذ کرنے میں مرتین کے حق کو باطل کرنا ہے تو یہ بیچ کے مشابہ ہو گیا بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ معتق مالدار ہو اس حیثیت سے کہ ان کے بعض اقوال پر عتق نافذ ہو جائے گا اس لئے کہ ضمان واجب ہونے کی وجہ معنی مرتہن کا حق باطل نہ ہوگا اور بخلاف مستاجر کو آزاد کرنے کے اس لئے کہ اجارہ اپنی مدت تک باقی رہیگا اس لئے کہ آزاد اجارہ کو قبول کرتا ہے بہر حال آزادر ہن کو قبول نہیں کرتا پس رہن باقی نہیں رہیگا۔ تشریح اگر راہن مرہون غلام کو فروخت کر دے تو اس کی بیع موقوف ہوتی ہے ( کما مر ) لیکن اگر راہن مرہون غلام کو آزاد کر دے تو کیا حکم ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ اس میں اختلاف ہے! احناف کا مسلک یہ ہے کہ معتق خواہ مالدار ہو خواہ غریب عشق نافذ ہوگا اور غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ دونوں صورتوں میں کچھ جدا گانہ احکام جاری ہوں گے مگر عشق درست ہے امام شافعی کے اس میں تین قول ہیں۔ - حنفیہ کے موافق کہ مطلق عشق نافذ ہے۔

زواله
ترجمہ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ مخاطب ہے ( عاقل بالغ ہے) جس نے اپنی ملک کو آزاد کیا ہے تو مرتہن کی اجازت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا تصرف لغو نہ ہوگا جیسے خریدے ہوئے غلام کو قبضہ سے پہلے آزاد کر دیا اور بھاگے ہوئے غلام یا مغصوب کو آزاد کر دیا اور ملک رقبہ
کے قیام میں کوئی پوشید گی نہیں ہے مقتضی کے قیام کی وجہ سے اور رہن کا عارض ہو نا زوال ملک کی خبر نہیں دیتا۔ تشریح ہم نے محق کو مطلق جائز و نافذ قرار دیا ہے جس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ راہن عاقل بالغ ہے جو احکام شرعیہ کا مخاطب و مکلف ہے اور وہ اپنے مملوک کو آزاد کر رہا ہے تو مرتہن کے اجازت نہ دینے کی وجہ سے اس کا تصرف لغو نہ ہوگا۔
سوال مالک تو اگر چہ را ہن ہے لیکن اس کا قبضہ تو نہیں ہے لہذ ا عتق نافذ نہ ہونا چاہیے؟ جواب قبضہ بیچ کے لئے ضروری تھا تا کہ تعلیم پر قدرت حاصل ہو جائے اور اعتماق میں تسلیم کی ضرورت ہی نہیں لہذا بغیر قبضہ کے عتق نافذ ہوگا جس کی تین مثالیں سنئے۔ زید نے ایک غلام بکر سے خریدا اور ابھی زید نے غلام پر قبضہ نہیں کیا اس سے پہلے ہی غلام کو آزاد کر دیا تو عتق نافذ ہے اگر چہ غلام پر
قبضہ نہیں ہے۔ -۲- زید کا غلام بھاگ گیا اور زید نے اس کو آزاد کر دیا تو عتق نافذ ہو جائے گا اگر چہ غلام اس کے قبضہ میں نہیں ہے۔ - زید نے بکر کا غلام غصب کر لیا اور بکر نے اس حال میں اس کو آزاد کر دیا تو عشق نافذ ہوگا حالانکہ ان تینوں مثالوں میں بوقت اعتاق
Book of Pledge. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidaya Volume 14. And in the second case, in the three contracts (lease, gift, pledge), the pledgee has no right because in pledge and gift there is no exchange, and in lease the rent is the exchange, but it is an exchange for benefit, not for the very property, and the pledgee's right is attached to the property, not to the benefit. Summary: the pledgee has no right in the three contracts, so his permission is not effective for specification, i.e., when the pledgee has lifted his right from the pledged property, the impediment to the pledgor's disposition is removed. Now we consider which contract to permit: we chose sale and permitted it. Why? Because it is primary, and because it is binding, and because it benefits the pledgee. Thus the difference is clear. The pledgor freed the pledged slave, will he be free or not? Statements of jurists. He (al-Quduri) said: If the pledgor frees the pledged slave, his freeing is effective. In some statements of al-Shafi'i, it is not effective if the freeer is insolvent, because in its implementation is the nullification of the pledgee's right, so it resembles sale. Contrary to if he is wealthy, where it is effective according to some of his statements, because his right is not effectively nullified due to compensation. And unlike the freeing of a lessee, because the lease remains for its term since freedom accepts the lease, but the pledge does not accept it, so the pledge does not remain. Translation: al-Quduri said: If the pledgor freed the pledged slave, his freeing is effective. And in some statements of al-Shafi'i it is not effective when the freeer is insolvent, because implementing it nullifies the pledgee's right, so it resembles sale. Contrary to when the freeer is wealthy, where according to some of his statements the freeing is effective because due to the obligation of compensation the pledgee's right is not effectively nullified. And unlike freeing a lessee, because the lease remains for its term since freedom accepts the lease, but freedom does not accept the pledge, so the pledge does not remain. Explanation: If the pledgor sells the pledged slave, his sale is suspended (as before). But if the pledgor frees the pledged slave, what is the ruling? He said there is disagreement. The Hanafi madhhab is that whether the freeer is wealthy or poor, the freeing is effective and the slave becomes free. Second point: in both cases some separate rulings apply, but the freeing is correct. Imam al-Shafi'i has three statements: (1) in agreement with Hanafis: absolute freeing is effective; (2) absolute freeing is not effective, whether wealthy or poor, because when his sale is not effective, then freeing is a fortiori not effective; (3) if the freeer (pledgor) is wealthy, freeing is effective, if poor, not effective. The author mentioned this and alluded to the other statements. The proof for non-effectiveness in the case of insolvency is that implementing his freeing nullifies the pledgee's right, so just as sale is not effective, similarly freeing will not be effective. And the proof for effectiveness in the case of wealth is that in this case the pledgor will have to pay compensation, so the pledgee's right is not nullified. Question: According to one statement of Imam al-Shafi'i, he does not consider the freeing of even a wealthy pledgor to be effective, and he gives the reason that it necessitates the nullification of the pledgee's right. But if a master gives his slave on lease and during the lease term the lessor (pledgor) frees that leased slave, his freeing is effective, even though it nullifies the lessee's right. What is the difference between the two? Answer: Yes, they permit freeing the lessee. The reason is that there is no conflict between freedom and lease; rather a free person can also be a lessee, so after freeing the lease contract remains in force for its term, so nullification does not necessarily occur. But between freedom and pledge there is conflict, meaning a free person cannot be pledged, so after freeing the pledge is also terminated, leading to the nullification of the pledgee's right. For this reason Imam al-Shafi'i considered the pledgor's freeing invalid and the lessor's freeing valid. The Hanafi proof: And our proof is that he is a responsible person (mukallaf) who freed his own property, so his disposition is not void due to lack of the pledgee's permission, just as if he freed a purchased slave before delivery, or freed a runaway or usurped slave. And there is no concealment in the existence of ownership of the corpus due to the existence of the cause, and the temporary impediment of pledge does not indicate its removal. Translation: And our proof is that he is a responsible person (sane adult) who freed his own property, so his disposition is not void due to the lack of the pledgee's permission, like freeing a purchased slave before delivery, and freeing a runaway or usurped slave. And there is no concealment in the existence of ownership of the corpus due to the existence of the cause, and the temporary impediment of pledge does not indicate its removal. Explanation: We have declared freeing absolutely valid and effective. The proof is that the pledgor is a sane adult who is addressed and obligated by the shari'ah, and he is freeing his own slave, so his disposition is not void due to the pledgee's lack of permission. Question: He is the owner but he does not have possession, so the freeing should not be effective? Answer: Possession was necessary for sale to enable delivery, but in freeing there is no need for delivery, so without possession the freeing is effective. Listen to three examples: (1) Zaid bought a slave from Bakr, and before Zaid took possession of the slave, he freed him. The freeing is effective even though he has no possession. (2) Zaid's slave ran away, and Zaid freed him; the freeing is effective even though the slave is not in his possession. (3) Zaid usurped Bakr's slave, and Bakr freed him in that state; the freeing is effective even though at the time of freeing the slave was not in his possession.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya deals with the ruling on a pledgor freeing a pledged slave. The author first establishes the framework: in the three contracts of lease (ijarah), gift (hibah), and pledge (rahn), the pledgee has no right to the property itself; his right is attached to the value of the pledged item. Therefore, if the pledgee releases his right, the pledgor's disposition becomes unhindered. The author then specifically addresses sale and freeing. The core issue is whether a pledgor can effectively free a pledged slave without the pledgee's consent. The Hanafi position, derived from the text of al-Quduri, is that the freeing is effective unconditionally. The Shafi'i school has three opinions: unconditional effectiveness, unconditional ineffectiveness, and conditional effectiveness based on the pledgor's wealth. The Hanafis argue that the pledgor is the legal owner of the slave (he owns the corpus, raqabah), and the pledge is merely a temporary encumbrance that does not negate ownership. Since the pledgor is a legally responsible person (mukallaf) and his action is an act of worship (freeing slaves is recommended), his disposition cannot be voided by the pledgee's lack of permission. The text gives analogies: freeing a purchased slave before delivery, a runaway slave, or a usurped slave—all cases where the owner does not have physical possession but the freeing is still valid. The Hanafis distinguish this from sale, where the pledgee's permission is required because sale transfers ownership and harms the pledgee's right to the corpus. In freeing, the pledgee's right is protected by compensation if the pledgor is wealthy. The passage also contrasts with lease: freeing a leased slave is valid because lease and freedom are compatible; the lease continues, but freeing a pledged slave terminates the pledge, harming the pledgee. Thus, the Shafi'i view on freeing a lessee is consistent with their analogy, but they do not extend it to pledge because of conflict. The Hanafi proof is clear: ownership of the corpus persists despite the pledge, and the pledge is a temporary hindrance that does not remove ownership, so the owner's actions are valid. The commentary emphasizes that this is an inter-madhhab dispute and the Hanafi position is based on the strength of ownership and the general principle that a free person's actions on their property are valid unless there is a specific prohibition.

Key Terms
رہن (rahn): A contract where a property is given as security for a debt.
مرتہن (murtahin): The person who receives the pledge as security, i.e., the creditor/pledgee.
راہن (rahin): The person who gives the pledge, i.e., the debtor/pledgor.
عتق (itq): The act of freeing a slave; manumission.
عبد مرہون (abd marhun): A slave that has been given as a pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a pledgor (rahn) frees a pledged slave?
A) The freeing is effective only if the pledgor is wealthy.
B) The freeing is effective only with the pledgee's consent.
C) The freeing is effective unconditionally, and the slave becomes free.
D) The freeing is not effective under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that a pledgor's freeing of a pledged slave is unconditionally effective because ownership of the corpus persists despite the pledge, and the pledgee's right is protected through compensation when the pledgor is wealthy.
